2004 WL 1739212 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIANA JIMENEZ REVILLA
File: AXX XX4 428 - Houston
July 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed by an Immigration Judge in an order dated August 9, 2002. This Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from such decision on December 16, 2003. The respondent has filed a motion to “reopen and reconsider,” arguing that her prior counsel was ineffective in that he failed to file a timely appeal brief on her behalf. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion.
 
We find that the respondent has failed to comply with the procedural requirements we set forth in our decision Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which governs our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between the respondent and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
The respondent has submitted a copy of a “Grievance Form” that she allegedly filed with the State Bar of Texas, in which she complains of her representation by her former counsel, Mr. Robert E. Kahn, Esquire. The respondent has informed the State Bar that Mr. Kahn “failed to appeal on time” which has “created a problem” for her “legal status.” See Grievance Form.
 
The respondent has complied with the Lozada requirements only insofar as she may have informed the appropriate disciplinary authorities of her grievance against her former counsel. Importantly, there is no evidence that the respondent has actually informed Mr. Kahn of the allegation she has made against him. Indeed, in her motion, the respondent asserts only that a copy of the complaint “will be served” on Mr. Kahn, not that it has been served on him so that he may have an opportunity to respond to the allegations.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent has failed to set forth, in detail, the agreement she had with Mr. Kahn. Notably, the minimal information the respondent allegedly provided to the State Bar appears to be incorrect. The respondent alleges in her complaint that Mr. Kahn did not file a timely appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision. However, a timely appeal was, in fact, filed on the respondent’s behalf. Instead, it was simply the appeal brief which counsel did not timely file, although he did subsequently attempt to file a “motion to remand” which essentially set forth the reasons why he believed the Immigration Judge’s decision was incorrect.
 
Accordingly, we find that the respondent has failed to comply with the Lozada procedural requirements. Moreover, even if the respondent had complied with the procedural requirements in support of her allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel, we find that the respondent has not adequately demonstrated that Mr. Kahn’s representation caused her any prejudice. It does not appear that the respondent has any relief available to her from removal in these proceedings. During the August 9, 2002, hearing, the respondent’s counsel informed the Immigration Judge that the respondent had an “E visa” application pending before the DHS, and that she should know within 3 or 4 months if such visa was approved (Tr. at 10-11). We note that neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board has any authority over the approval of such visas. In addition, even though almost 2 years have passed since such hearing, the respondent has never informed the Board of the status of her petition before the DHS.
 
In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1739182 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEBASTIANA GONCALVES-LOPES DE SILVA
File: AXX XX5 810 - Las Vegas
July 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ligia M. Madeira, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 29, 2002, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings and to change venue. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on May 23, 2002. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal and in her motion to reopen, the respondent contends that she demonstrated exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear, based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the respondent contends that she did not receive notice from her former attorney, Paul Glickman. The respondent indicated that she only communicated with a Brazilian paralegal, Mr. Alves, who acted as an interpreter for the respondent at a meeting with Mr. Glickman. Apparently all parties agreed that Mr. Alves would act as a liaison for the respondent since she was unable to communicate with Mr. Glickman in English. The respondent gave $500 to Mr. Alves, who failed to pay Mr. Glickman for further representation of the respondent before the Immigration Court. Consequently, since Mr. Glickman had not yet received his retainer, he was no longer working on her case. The respondent contends that neither Mr. Alves nor Mr. Glickman provided her with notice of her hearing scheduled for May 23, 2002. With her motion to reopen, the respondent submitted numerous documents, including her affidavit and Paul Glickman’s affidavit.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, supra.
 
Precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this case arises, provides that while the requirements of Lozada are generally reasonable, they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means. Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240 n.6 (9th Cir. 2000). The Ninth Circuit determined that “Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra. Furthermore, where the face of the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel, there has been substantial compliance with the rule. Id.
 
*2 The record in the instant case supports the conclusion that based on the totality of circumstances, the respondent has established exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. It appears that due to the actions of Mr. Alves, Mr. Glickman did not receive his retainer, and he subsequently ceased representing the respondent. Due to her inability to communicate with Mr. Glickman, and the actions of Mr. Alves, the respondent did not receive notice of her hearing. We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The record will be remanded for further proceedings, and the motion to change venue will be granted.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to change venue is granted, and venue is changed from Las Vegas, Nevada, to Boston, Massachusetts.

FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2004 WL 1739053 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GUADALUPE MALDONADO GARCIA LETICIA RANGEL TORRES
Files: AXX XX1 687 - Los Angeles
AXX XX1 656
July 7, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Jessica Dominguez, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on December 24, 2003, when we summarily dismissed the appeal pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4). The respondents have timely filed a motion to reopen. They argue that ineffective assistance of counsel caused their application for cancellation of removal to be denied. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen may not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). A motion to reopen may not be granted if the respondent fails to establish a prima facie case for the relief sought. Matter of Coelho, supra; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Furthermore, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. Id. (citations omitted).
 
Although the respondents provided an affidavit setting forth their complaints (Motion to Reopen, Exh. 2) and attached a copy of a complaint form (Motion to Reopen, Exh. 3), there is no evidence that the respondents’ former attorney was informed of the allegations leveled against him or that he was given an opportunity to respond. As such, the respondents have failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 Assuming that the Matter of Lozada, supra, requirements were met, the respondents have failed to show that they were prejudiced by their former representative’s performance. The respondents allege that their former attorney failed to highlight the scholastic success of their son. The basis for the motion, aside from the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, is a letter dated January 5, 2004, indicating that the respondents’ son has now been placed in a “high achiever classroom” (Motion to Reopen, Exh. 4). However, the fact that their son is a sixth grade student who has been placed in a high achiever classroom is insufficient to satisfy the exceptional and extremely unusual hardship standard imposed by Congress. Furthermore, the respondents’ former attorney submitted the scholastic records of their son (AXX-XXX-XXX, Exh. 2 at 13, 34, 37; AXX-XXX-XXX, Exh. 2a at 124), but in prior grades he did not do as well as he is now doing. Since the respondents failed to show that they were prejudiced by their former counsel’s performance, they failed to meet the requirements necessary to reopen a case based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The following order will issue.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 1739053 (BIA)
End of Document

2004 WL 1739055 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EFRAIN RAMIREZ-LEMUS
File: A71 558 095 - San Antonio
July 7, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda Irene Perez, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered orally on October 21, 2003.1 The appeal was accordingly due on or before November 20, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on May 26, 2004. We accordingly find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent filed a Respondent’s Motion to Reopen Proceedings in which the respondent is raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as the reason for the untimeliness of the appeal. A State Bar of Texas Grievance Form that was filed with the State Bar of Texas on April 15, 2004 was provided as evidence to support this claim. However, we find that this evidence alone is not enough, and, therefore, we need not address the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel considering the respondent has not fully complied with the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.3(a), 3.38, 3.39, 240.14 and 240.15.
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, Interim Decision 3343 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § § 3.2(c)(2), 3.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

2004 WL 1739136 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOSCHAVEL MORA
File: AXX XX6 084 - Houston
June 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lawrence E. Rushton, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on February 25, 2004, when we summarily affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal for failure to timely file the application. On May 5, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen.
 
A motion to reopen before the Board seeks a second review of a case by the Board based on new or previously unavailable evidence. The motion must state the new facts to be proved and must be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). A motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. Id. The decision whether to grant a motion to reopen is within the discretion of the Board. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel, Robert E. Kahn. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Specifically, the respondent contends that Mr. Kahn failed to pursue all forms of relief available to the respondent by not submitting an application on her behalf as a derivative beneficiary pursuant to the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act (NACARA), although she is the spouse of a NACARA applicant who obtained his lawful permanent residency. She also contends that Mr. Kahn advised her to pursue the relief of cancellation of removal for a non-lawful permanent resident, but he failed to timely file her application by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge, resulting in the Immigration Judge’s finding that her application for cancellation of removal had been abandoned. The respondent states that Mr. Kahn has been contacted regarding the allegations and has submitted a response from him. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exh. E. Furthermore, the respondent has submitted evidence that she has filed a grievance against Mr. Kahn with the State Bar of Texas. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exh. F.
 
In the case at bar, the respondent’s former attorney has admitted that the application for cancellation was not timely filed, and he regrets that the respondent’s case was pretermitted due to his error. We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and the proceedings will be reopened and remanded for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

2004 WL 1739160 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSENDO REYES HERRERA
File: AXX XX1 061 - Los Angeles
June 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John Ayala, Esquire
*1 An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on March 22, 2000. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on September 18, 2002. The respondent thereafter sought to have proceedings reopened in order to pursue an application for adjustment of status, based on marriage to a United States citizen. We denied the motion to reopen on August 25, 2003, finding that relief was barred because the respondent failed to voluntarily depart the United States as ordered on September 18, 2002. See section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d)(alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief, including adjustment of status). The respondent has filed another motion to reopen on January 23, 2004, blaming former counsel for the earlier motion being denied.
 
We will grant the pending motion, and remand the case to the Immigration Judge for additional proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion.
 
Although the motion to reopen was filed late, and was the second motion to reopen filed, we have determined that it is appropriate to reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent asserts that he was told that an attorney would enter an appearance with this Board prior to our September 18, 2002, decision. The respondent then moved and was promised that counsel would notify the Board of his new address. The respondent asserts that the attorney did not inform the Board of his address change, and failed to enter an appearance prior to our September 18, 2002, decision. The respondent asserts that the attorney then filed the first motion to reopen without his knowledge. The respondent states that he was unaware of either of our prior decisions until December, 2003, and the pending motion was filed the next month. The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The immediate relative visa petition was approved on October 23, 2001. On remand to the Immigration Judge the respondent will have an opportunity to present his application for relief. The DHS shall have the opportunity to examine and verify the validity of the respondent’s submissions and present evidence.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s pending motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s order of August 25, 2003, is rescinded.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2004 WL 1739050 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOIE LIONG LOUW PJK KHING OEY JESSICA LOUW JUSTIN LOUW
Files: A76 142 999 - New York
A76 143 000
A76 143 001
A76 143 002
June 25, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Neil H. Afran, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On October 8, 2002, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondents removed in absentia. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). On April 8, 2003, the respondents filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging they had failed to appear due to the advice of a non-attorney who had fraudulently claimed to be an attorney (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on April 25, 2003, and the respondents have now appealed from that decision. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
The Immigration Judge held that the respondents failed to meet the documentary requirements for a motion to reopen based on the ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 3).1 See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The respondents have documented their claim against Karen Fotiou, who fraudulently claimed to be an attorney and incorrectly advised the respondents they did not need to appear for their October 8, 2002, hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to withdraw of the attorney the respondents had retained before hiring Fotiou, however, and an attorney named David Hawkins attempted to enter an appearance on behalf of the respondents at their October 8 hearing for Fotiou. Under these circumstances, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s holding that the respondents need to satisfy the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance against these attorneys as well, and not just Fotiou (I.J. at 3). The respondents have not addressed their failure to do so in their appeal.
 
We note that the respondents’ first attorney’s motion to withdraw appears to have met the requirements for such a motion. See Matter of Rosales, 19 I&N Dec. 655 (BIA 1988). Nevertheless, the attorney remained the respondents’ attorney of record after the denial of her motion to withdraw and, consequently, is also implicated in the respondents’ claim of exceptional circumstances for having failed to appear at their October 8, 2002, hearing. We disagree with the respondents’ assertion that a transcript is required for adequate review of the respondents’ appeal. Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal for the respondents’ failure to satisfy the requirements of Matter of Lozada for both their attorney of record at the time of their October 8 hearing and the attorney that attempted to enter an appearance on their behalf at that hearing.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 1739167 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAZHAR BECHIR MHIRI
File: AXX XX9 958 - Arlington
June 25, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ashraf W. Nubani, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Graciela Vazquez de Schwartz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; remand.
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of Tunisia. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s December 5, 2002, decision finding the respondent removable as charged and denying his motion for a continuance in order to file an I-140 petition. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to remand will be denied. The request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e).
 
On appeal, the respondent avers that he did not have the opportunity to explain his case to the Immigration Judge. However, the record reflects that the respondent did explain his case to the Immigration Judge, during four separate hearings, over a period of 15 months. The record also reflects that the Immigration Judge granted the respondent three continuances, during that period, in order to allow the respondent to pursue a diversity visa, an I-130 visa petition, and an I-140 visa petition. The respondent and the counsel from the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) realized that there had been multiple problems with the respondent’s pursuit of his diversity visa, thus, both parties agreed to adjudicate the respondent’s adjustment application in court. See Tr. at 13. The Immigration Judge informed the respondent that he would need to retrieve his documents, relating to his adjustment application, from DHS and have them ready for his next hearing. See Tr. at 14. The counsel for DHS informed the respondent’s counsel that it would not agree to a motion to terminate in order for the respondent to pursue the diversity visa. Id. However, 9 months later the respondent came to court without the requested documents and indicated that he had failed to file his I-140, although he knew that his diversity visa was not approved by the September, 2002 deadline. See Tr. at 17. As the respondent had already been granted prior continuances, over a period of 15 months, we do not find that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by failing to grant the respondent an additional continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6.
 
In addition, we note that the respondent’s counsel was told, by the Immigration Judge, that the respondent’s motion for a further continuance was denied and that he needed to qualify the respondent for voluntary departure. See Tr. at 18. However, the respondent’s counsel continued to contest the denial of his motion for a continuance. Id. The Immigration Judge asked the respondent’s counsel if he understood the Court’s order, which was that the motion for a continuance was denied and that he must qualify the respondent for voluntary departure. Id. The respondent’s counsel replied in the affirmative and then continued to contest the denial of his motion for a continuance. Id. The Immigration Judge again indicated that the respondent’s motion was denied and instructed the respondent’s counsel to qualify the respondent for voluntary departure. See Tr. at 19. The respondent’s counsel failed to do so and the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed as charged. Id. We do not find that the Immigration Judge erred in ordering the respondent removed after the respondent’s counsel failed to qualify the respondent for voluntary departure. We note that the respondent has not claimed that he received ineffective assistance of counsel according to the requirements delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion to remand is denied.

2004 WL 1739067 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LI, MEI YING
File: AXX XX9 789 - New York
June 24, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Cox, Theodore N., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Egan,
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated January 3, 2003. The respondent asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and also requests reopening pursuant to the Board’s sua sponte authority. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s motion.
 
At the outset, we note that the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel appears to be based on allegations against both Mr. Andrew J. Wilson, her former attorney of record before the Immigration Court, and Mr. Xiong He of AmeProfessional Group. However, the record does not indicate that Mr. He or the AmeProfessional Group had at any time acted as the respondent’s legal representative, nor has the respondent offered any evidence to establish that they are in any way affiliated with her former counsel. As such, with respect to the respondent’s Lozada claim, we find it appropriate to limit our consideration to solely the respondent’s allegations against Mr. Wilson, her former attorney of record.
 
We find that the respondent has failed to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Specifically, since the record indicates that notice to Mr. Wilson was mailed on the same day that the respondent’s motion was submitted to the Board (see Exh. A of Motion), we cannot reasonably conclude that the respondent has complied with the requirement that “before allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (emphasis added). Moreover, we are not convinced that the respondent’s former counsel was sufficiently informed of the allegations against him, based on the brief description of the respondent’s allegations in the letter to former counsel (see Exh. A of Motion. There is no indication that the respondent attached to the letter to Mr. Wilson any additional information relating to her allegations). We further note that in her affidavit, the respondent has asserted only general allegations that her former counsel “did not review or prepare the case” with her (see Respondent’s Affidavit in Exh. A to Motion). The respondent has submitted no additional evidence to support these allegations, or to demonstrate how counsel’s conduct may have affected the outcome of her case. Furthermore, in our review of the record, we find that the respondent received a fair and complete hearing before the Immigration Judge, and was provided ample opportunity to present her claim for relief. In all, we cannot conclude that respondent has established that she has suffered prejudice as a result of former counsel’s conduct, such that it is likely that she would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Matter of Aassad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640.
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to satisfy the requirements for proving that she was ineffectively represented or was prejudiced by her former counsel in these proceedings. We further find that the respondent has failed to state any “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant our granting her motion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.

We find that the respondent has failed to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Specifically, since the record indicates that notice to Mr. Wilson was mailed on the same day that the respondent’s motion was submitted to the Board (see Exh. A of Motion), we cannot reasonably conclude that the respondent has complied with the requirement that “before allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (emphasis added). 2004 WL 1739063 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IGNACIO MARTINEZ GARCIA
File: AXX XX3 887 - Los Angeles
June 23, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raul Gomez, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge dated May 12, 2003. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent failed to establish the requisite continuous physical presence for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). See section 240A(b)(1)(A) of the Act. Moreover, the respondent failed to show that his two United States citizen children would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if he is returned to Mexico (Tr. at 10-12, 18-19). See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act.
 
On appeal, the respondent apparently alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel. In Lozada, we laid out the requirements that the respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. First, the motion should be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts. Second, before the allegation is presented to the Board, the former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion. Finally, if it is asserted that prior counsel’s handling of the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). The Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to generally be reasonable, but has held that “they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). Here, however, the respondent has failed to even minimally fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. As such, reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
Additionally, the respondent has failed to show prejudice. “Due process challenges to deportation proceedings require a showing of prejudice to succeed.” Castillo-Perez v. I.N.S., 212 F.3d 518, 527 (9th Cir. 2000). “Prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (quoting Barraza Rivera v. INS, 913 F.2d 1443, 1448 (9th Cir. 1990)). The respondent here has not met this requirement. Specifically, the respondent has failed to proffer what evidence his former counsel failed to submit regarding the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal. Consequently, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel’s performance was so inadequate that it may have changed the outcome of the proceedings.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 1739096 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABDALLAH JAMIL KOURANI
File: AXX XX5 979 - New York
June 18, 2004
 
IN EXCLUSION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF APPLICANT:
 
Neil Richardson Datta, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mary Cheng
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The case was last before the Board on March 8, 1996, when we dismissed the applicant’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the applicant excludable and ordering him deported. The applicant has filed a motion to reopen exclusion proceedings. The motion will be denied.
 
The applicant’s motion asserts that he had reasonable cause for failing to appear at his exclusion hearing because he did not receive notice. He also claims that due to ineffective assistance of counsel prior counsel failed to properly pursue his appeal with the Board.
 
The applicant’s argument that he had a reasonable excuse for failing to appear due to a lack of notice will be construed as a motion to reconsider since we previously addressed the argument in our decision dated March 8, 1996. A motion to reconsider a decision must be filed with the Board within 30 days after the mailing of the Board decision or on or before July 31, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). In this case, the motion was filed on May 12, 2004, more than 8 years after the Board’s decision. As such, the applicant’s motion is untimely.
 
Even if the argument had been timely raised, it is without merit. A motion to reconsider requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Upon review of the motion to reconsider and record of proceedings, we find no new legal argument or particular aspect of the case which was overlooked and no ground upon which to reconsider our previous decision. In fact, the applicant’s argument completely fails to address the evidence of record reflecting that the applicant was personally served with a written hearing notice advising him of his next hearing. Accordingly, the applicant’s argument construed as a motion to reconsider, is denied.
 
The applicant’s motion also asserts that his case should be reopened due to ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); cf. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Furthermore, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
*2 While the applicant has submitted an affidavit, the affidavit is contradicted by evidence contained in the record of proceedings. Specifically, the applicant’s affidavit asserts that he retained the law firm of Liz & Botshon in August of 1993. However, a letter from the applicant’s counsel, dated November 9, 1993, requested an extension to file the applicant’s appeal brief because the firm had “just been retained.” Furthermore, the affidavit does not identify the name of the counsel who allegedly provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Thus, the affidavit is insufficient.
 
Lozada also requires that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations and given an opportunity to respond. However, the motion reflects that counsel was informed of the allegations on the same day as the motion was filed with the Board. Thus, counsel was not afforded an opportunity to respond. Furthermore, the complaint filed with the state disciplinary authorities failed to identify a specific attorney that rendered inadequate representation. Moreover, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s performance. While he claims that his appeal would not have been denied if his counsel had filed an affidavit, the Board’s prior decision did not rely solely on the lack of an affidavit as a basis for dismissing the appeal. The Board clearly stated that the record revealed that the applicant had in fact been advised in writing of his hearing and the consequences of failing to appear. Thus, the applicant has failed to establish that his motion should be granted pursuant to Lozada.
 
In addition, the applicant has failed to establish that his motion is subject to equitable tolling because he has not demonstrated due diligence. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000) (filing deadline of motion to reopen may be equitably tolled where alien demonstrated due diligence). Specifically, the applicant failed to file his motion to reopen until more than 10 years after he encountered problems with his prior counsel. Thus, the applicant’s motion to reopen is untimely filed, See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
ORDER: The applicant’s motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 1739096 (BIA)

2004 WL 1739119 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR MENDEZ CHAVEZ
File: AXX XX0 680 - Los Angeles
June 17, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael S. Cabrera, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge issued a final order of removal against the respondent on March 21, 2003. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38. The appeal was filed late, on April 23, 2003. The respondent filed with his appeal a “Motion For Late Acceptance of Untimely Filed Notice of Appeal.” The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the respondent’s filings.
 
The respondent urges the Board to accept the appeal out of time. The respondent claims that he thought former counsel would file the appeal for him, but the attorney failed to do so. However, the respondent has failed to meet any of the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Most critically, the respondent must show prejudice resulting from his attorney’s actions. Matter of Assaad, supra, at 556, 561-62; Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638; Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The Immigration Judge found the respondent to be ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(b)(1). The Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal, as he was convicted of an offense under section 212(a)(2) of the Act. See section 240A(b)(1)(C) of the Act. Specifically, the respondent was convicted on April 27, 2001, of possession for sale of marijuana, with the sentence suspended. See section 101(a)(48)(B) of the Act (term of imprisonment refers to the period of incarceration imposed by court regardless of a partial or total suspension thereof).
 
The respondent admitted to his crime at the removal hearing, and admitted that it made him ineligible for relief (Tr. at 17). The respondent states that he is seeking to have his conviction vacated. However, we have found that the filing of a post-conviction motion or other form of collateral attack does not affect the finality of convictions for immigration purposes, unless and until the conviction has been overturned pursuant to such a motion. See Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750, 752 (BIA 1993).
 
*2 The respondent is therefore ineligible for relief. We therefore find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31 (1999).

2004 WL 1739110 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ATANAS APOSTOLOV GENOV
File: AXX XX2 516 - Chicago
June 15, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mary L. Sfasciotti, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph M. Yeung
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case is presently before us pursuant to the April 8, 2004, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit granting the government’s motion to remand. The government requested a remand solely with regard to this Board’s August 29, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The government motion noted that in denying reconsideration, we stated that the respondent had not supported his previously filed motion to reopen to seek suspension of deportation with the required application for relief. However, the respondent had in fact filed a suspension application before the Immigration Judge, on November 13, 1997. The government’s motion also states that on remand, the Board can decide as well whether or not to rule on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent’s motion to reconsider will now be granted and the deportation proceedings will be reopened.
 
As indicated above, the respondent had previously filed the required application for suspension of deportation. The Immigration Judge denied that application on February 27, 1998. He found that the respondent satisfied the continuous physical presence requirement under the special suspension provisions of the Nicaraguan and Central American Relief Act of 1997, Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193, 2196 (NACARA). He further found that the respondent was of good moral character, but he concluded that the respondent, who had then been in the United States for less than 8 years and had no qualifying relatives here, had not established the requisite extreme hardship for suspension.
 
Since the time of the 1998 hearing, the respondent has married his long-time girlfriend, a lawful permanent resident of the United States. She filed a visa petition on the respondent’s behalf on April 30, 2001. The respondent also has now lived here for over 14 years, more than twice the 7 years required for suspension of deportation. He has accumulated significant assets here, and apparently has been employed and a productive, hard-working member of society. Under these circumstances, we find that the respondent should have his proceedings reopened for further consideration of his application for suspension.
 
We further find that the respondent’s second motion to reopen is not number barred because he has shown ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see generally Chowdhury v. Ashcroft, 241 F.3d 848 (7th Cir. 2001). We note that the respondent and his present counsel have presented detailed affidavits regarding their unsuccessful efforts to contact the respondent’s prior counsel, and have otherwise complied with the Lozada requirements. Further, prior counsel’s advice that the respondent not marry his permanent resident girlfriend, and his failure to support the respondent’s first motion to reopen with the required documents, or to timely inform the respondent of the Board’s November 7, 2002, decision denying reopening, constitute ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Under all these circumstances, we find that respondent’s deportation proceedings should be reopened. Accordingly, the decisions of the Board dated August 29, 2003, and May 16, 2003, are vacated, and the motion to reconsider is granted. Upon reconsideration, the deportation proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for a further hearing.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1739099 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE FELIPE FRANCO
File: A70 531 865 - Miami
June 7, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eduardo Soto, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is appealing the Immigration Judge’s written decision dated March 19, 2003, in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his deportation proceedings. The respondent had previously been ordered deported to Guatemala in absentia on February 1, 1995. The respondent has not filed a brief in support of his appeal even though he indicated on his Notice of Appeal, Form EOIR-26, that a brief would be forthcoming. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not filed an appeal brief.
 
We affirm and adopt the Immigration Judge’s decision and with the following comments we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent asserts that he complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney, Mr. Carlos Alberto Rodriguez. The respondent further argues that the fact he waited approximately 7 years to advance an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in conjunction with a motion to reopen should not cause doubt to be cast upon his allegation. See Notice of Appeal, Form EOIR-26.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly implied that merely complying with the procedural requirements set forth in Lozada does not establish that an alien’s prior counsel was ineffective. It does not appear that the procedural requirements of Lozada have even been met in this case as there is little information from the respondent himself in his motion which can be fairly read to set forth “in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. The respondent has alleged only that his prior counsel informed him of an incorrect time to appear before the Immigration Court. See The Florida Bar Inquiry/Complaint Form.
 
In any event, even if the respondent were deemed to have complied with the Lozada procedural requirements, he still must establish that his prior counsel’s representation was, in fact, ineffective, and that such ineffectiveness caused him prejudice. Id. at 640. There is no explanation as to how the respondent recalls why exactly he failed to appear for his hearing so many years after such events transpired. Nor is there any reasonable explanation provided why the respondent waited so long to file his motion as he has been aware of his in absentia deportation order for several years. We agree with the Immigration Judge that this diminishes the reasonableness of his assertion.
 
*2 Indeed, the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen with the assistance of his prior counsel, and the respondent claimed at that time that he failed to appear for the hearing because he was “confused” as to the location of his hearing. See Motion to Reopen (received February 2, 1995).1 Thus, the respondent’s assertion why he failed to appear has shifted over the years and his belated claim of ineffective of assistance of counsel is unconvincing. Moreover, it does not appear that Mr. Rodriguez, his prior counsel, was necessarily ineffective as he had successfully obtained a motion for a continuance on the respondent’s behalf, appeared at the hearing for which the respondent failed to appear, and then filed a motion to reopen on his behalf.
 
Accordingly, in light of the foregoing discussion, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 1738966 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AI ZHENG CHEN A.K.A. OY KOON CHAN
File: AXX XX4 595 - New York City
June 3, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael A.O. Brown, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s February 3, 2003, decision denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be sustained. The record will be remanded.
 
On October 8, 1999, the respondent failed to appear for his removal hearing, and was ordered removed in absentia. A motion to reopen was filed on February 11, 2000, asserting that he was late for his hearing because he was waiting for his attorney, who was engaged in a telephonic master calendar. He and his attorney eventually appeared for the hearing 90 minutes late, and after an in absentia order of removal had been entered by the Immigration Judge. On June 8, 2000, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s February 11, 2000, motion to reopen, concluding that no exceptional circumstances were present. The respondent appealed, and this Board dismissed the appeal on February 11, 2002.
 
On January 22, 2003, the respondent made a new motion to reopen, asserting that he had recently discovered that his former attorney had ineffectively represented him, and requesting that proceedings be reopened. He argued that not only was he victimized by his attorney’s negligence on his hearing day, but that his counsel committed additional negligence in the manner in which he pursued reopening the respondent’s case. He further noted in that motion that his former attorney had lost his license to practice law. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that it was untimely.
 
Upon our review of the record, we find that the respondent’s motion is both time and numerically barred from consideration. See Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998); Matter of A-A, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) (holding that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act on the basis of exceptional circumstances). Although the Second Circuit, in which this case arises, has recognized the doctrine of “equitable tolling” in cases involving ineffective assistance of counsel, it has done so only when an alien has demonstrated that his or her constitutional right to due process was violated by the conduct of counsel, and when the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000) (concluding that the alien failed to demonstrate due diligence and thus, the doctrine of equitable tolling did not apply).
 
In the instant case, we have reviewed the documents submitted with the instant motion and find that the respondent may have been the victim of negligence. We further note that the respondent has attempted to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988, aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). However, the respondent must also demonstrate that he has suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Furthermore, he must demonstrate that he exercised due diligence in accordance with Iavorski v. INS, supra, before he can establish that equitable tolling of the statute of limitations is appropriate. Accordingly, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge to permit the Immigration Judge to determine whether the respondent has demonstrated that he exercised due diligence in accordance with Second Circuit law, and whether his constitutional right to due process was violated by the conduct of his counsel. Only then does the determination of compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, and Matter of Assaad, supra, become necessary.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained and his motion to reopen is granted. The record is remanded for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2004 WL 1739006 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE QUINONES - SEDILLO
File: A34 284 365 - San Antonio
April 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anne D. Monahan, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Allen Reid Tilson
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. These proceedings were last before us on February 18, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent now moves to reopen and reconsider these proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion is denied.
 
At the respondent’s hearing, the Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal when the respondent failed to file his fingerprints as required. The respondent has claimed that his representative failed to inform him in a timely manner of his obligation to file fingerprints for his application for cancellation. The respondent’s second attorney appealed the decision but did not address the ineffective assistance of counsel issues. The respondent’s third attorney has addressed ineffectiveness of counsel, but has failed to file sufficient documentation to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). For example, the respondent, now represented by his third attorney, has not stated whether a complaint was filed with the disciplinary authorities. Second, we see no evidence beyond statements of the third attorney,1 that the respondent informed the first attorney of his complaint. Also, the respondent has submitted a statement which contradicts in part his testimony about whether or not he was told to get his fingerprints taken; it also does not indicate how he was prejudiced. The respondent has not claimed that he was told a falsehood. Cf. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996).
 
We further find that the respondent did not establish that he is prima facie eligible for the relief he seeks. His conviction for committing domestic violence and several convictions for driving while intoxicated may support a finding that the respondent is not eligible in the exercise of discretion. Finally, we note that the respondent was informed of the necessity of departing the United States under the Immigration Judge’s order granting voluntary departure, since failing to leave would result in the denial of a number of kinds of relief. The respondent did not leave. The filing of a motion does not toll the voluntary departure period. Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996).

Unsworn attorney statements in briefs or motions do not constitute evidence and are not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n. 6 (1984); Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998); Matter of Obaigbena, 19 I&N Dec. 533, 534 n.2 (BIA 1988).

2004 WL 1739026 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSA ELVIA ZAMUNDIO-LOPEZ FERNANDO LEGIS-ZAMUNDIO MARIA DE LOS ANGELES LEGIS-ZAMUNDIO
Files: A76 610 962 - Los Angeles
A76 610 963
A76 610 964
April 23, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se1
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal will be dismissed.2 The record reflects that the respondents waived appeal. The summary of the Immigration Judge’s oral decision for each respondent dated January 31, 2000, reflects that they waived appeal. The transcript page 56, line 20, also reflects that the respondents’ waived appeal. However, on August 25, 2000, the respondents attempted to file a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), which was rejected for lack of proof of service. The respondents each resubmitted the Notice of Appeal and argued that their prior counsel did not advise them of the consequences of accepting a grant of voluntary departure in lieu of removal.
 
Upon review, the Board concludes that the respondents’ decision to waive appeal was knowingly and intelligently made. The record reveals that Immigration Judge discussed the requirements and consequences of voluntary departure prior to the completion of proceedings pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a) (Tr. at 50-54). The Immigration Judge briefly continued proceedings while the respondents’ counsel discussed the issue of voluntary departure prior to their waiver of appeal (Tr. at 53). The Board is satisfied that the waiver of appeal was expressly made by the respondents. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondents’ waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993).
 
In addition, the Board notes that the respondents attempted to make a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondents have not complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada,19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, as noted above, review of the record reveals that counsel discussed voluntary departure with his clients. The record before the Board simply does not support a finding that the respondents received ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge.
 
Accordingly, for these reasons, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1398686 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CLARA FERNANDA MARIN
File: AXX XX3 469 - Miami
April 20, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicolas A. Olano, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 5, 2003, decision denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on November 26, 2002. The request for oral argument is denied. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent failed to establish “exceptional circumstances” for her failure to appear at her scheduled removal hearing on November 26, 2002, such that rescission of the in absentia order was warranted. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act. This Board has held that an order of deportation may be rescinded where the alien properly establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel which amounts to “exceptional circumstances” within the meaning of the Act. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (B1A 1996); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent in this case failed to appear at her hearing because she sought advice from an immigration consultant, Mr. James Cook, upon receiving the notice of hearing. Mr. Cook apparently had been assisting her with the visa petition her United States citizen husband wished to file on her behalf, and she contends that he advised her to write a letter to the Immigration Court instead of appearing for the hearing. Unlike the cases cited by the respondent in support of her motion, where courts have found that the time limits for filing a motion to reopen may be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel when aliens are defrauded by persons claiming to be attorneys, the respondent has never claimed that Mr. Cook represented himself to be an attorney. Under these circumstances, it was unreasonable for her to disregard the hearing notice, relying instead on the advice of a person who was not, and did not represent himself to be, an attorney. Compare Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943, 946-47 (9th Cir. 1999), with Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000), and Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). For this reason, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at the scheduled hearing. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WALID HASSAN TAMAN A.K.A WALTER HASSAN TAMAN
File: A78 330 707 - Buffalo
April 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel M. Griebel
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. Sec.237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237 (a)(3)(D) I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(D)º - False claim of United States citizenship
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and Citizen of Egypt, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2003, denial of his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the appeal and the motion to reopen. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent claims that he failed to appear at his master calendar hearing on March 14, 2003, because his attorney’s office called him on March 13, 2003, and told him that the hearing had been cancelled due to a scheduling conflict. Respondent’s letter to the Immigration Judge, 3/26/03 (“First Letter”); Respondent’s Affidavit. When the respondent received the in absentia order, he called the court and was told by the clerk to write a letter to the judge with an explanation. The respondent did so, and the Immigration Judge treated the letter as a motion to reopen. On March 28, 2003, the motion was denied. On April 4, 2003, the respondent timely appealed.
 
In addition to his appeal, the respondent filed a second letter with the Immigration Court, explaining that his first letter was not meant to be a motion to reopen and that he had not otherwise filed a motion to reopen. The letter states that “¿the first letterº was not by any means a motion to reopen my proceeding, especially that I know that there are some rules that must be followed before I can file a motion to reopen my case.” Respondent’s letter to the Immigration Judge, April 7, 2003 (“Second Letter”). The Immigration Judge did not respond to the respondent’s second letter.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should not have considered his first letter to be a motion to reopen, especially in light of his second letter which explicitly stated that his first letter was not a motion to reopen. We agree.
 
While it was reasonable for the Immigration Judge to the treat the respondent’s first letter as a motion to reopen, his second letter should have been treated as a motion to reconsider due to the alleged error of fact that his first letter was not a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2) (2003) (stating that “¿aº motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision ….”). Upon reconsideration, the Immigration Judge should have vacated the motion denial since the respondent had not intended the first letter to be a motion. Absent such action, the respondent was barred from filing a proper motion to rescind. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(4)(ii) (allowing an alien ordered removed in absentia in removal proceedings to file only one motion to reopen).
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we find it appropriate to remand this case to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s appeal papers as his motion to rescind pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(4)(ii). On remand, the Immigration Judge should consider the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, which is supported by the appropriate documentation,1 as an exceptional circumstance for his failure to appeal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinon.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, an alien is not required to show prejudice in order to rescind an order of deportation entered following a hearing conducted in absentia under section 242B(c)(3) of the Act. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 473 n.2 (BIA 1996).

2004 WL 1398750 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERAFIN GONZALEZ GUADALUPE CHAVEZ
File: A95 443 512 - Los Angeles
A95 443 513
April 6, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica M. Little
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
In a decision dated April 3, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ motion to reopen alleging that their application for cancellation of removal, previously deemed abandoned by the Immigration Judge on March 5, 2003, had not been timely filed due to a calendaring error by the respondents’ former counsel. The Immigration Judge found that the respondents had failed to meet the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988),1 and, therefore, denied the motion.
 
Pursuant to precedent established by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the procedural requirements established under Matter of Lozada need not be met where “counsel’s ineffective assistance was obvious and undisputed on the face of the record.” Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126, 1130 (9th Cir. 2003) (citing Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000)). In the respondents’ motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, which includes a declaration from one of the attorneys handling the respondents’ case, it is clear that the failure to file a timely application for relief was the fault of counsel. The declaration states that the attorney had taken over calendaring for the law firm but due to unfamiliarity with the system, missed the deadline for filing the application for cancellation of removal. As the respondents appear prima facie eligible for the relief they seek, we find their attorney’s failure to file a timely application caused clear prejudice to the respondents and, therefore, will reverse the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (“¿p ºrejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” (quoting Castillo-Perez, supra at 527 n.12 (quoting Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999)))).
 
*2 ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondents’ motion to reopen is vacated and the record of proceedings is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada, supra holds that a motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not.
