KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Borges v. Gonzales, 3rd Cir., March 30, 2005
2004 WL 848509 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE A. BORGES
File: AXX XX1 940 - New York
March 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul O’Dwyer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - Nonimmigrant - remained longer than permitted
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and reopening
 
In a decision dated April 17, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent has appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
A brief procedural chronology follows. On February 3, 1998, an Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal against the respondent when he failed to appear for the scheduled hearing. On May 21, 1998, the respondent, through former counsel, moved to reconsider the decision. On June 10, 1998, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion finding that the respondent had failed to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. On January 24, 2003, the respondent, through present counsel, filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of his former counsel. On March 10, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion for two reasons. First, the motion was found to be untimely. Second, the Immigration Judge held that the respondent had failed to comply with the procedural requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). On April 2, 2003, the respondent moved to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s decision, and on April 17, 2003, the motion was denied because it was untimely filed, and because of the Lozada defect.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen due to non-compliance with Matter of Lozada. An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), maybe rescinded only upon a motion to reopen, filed within 180 days, which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The Immigration Judge correctly determined in denying the motion to reopen that the motion was untimely filed, and that in absentia proceedings were properly conducted.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims that an individual in his former counsel’s office told him that he did not have to appear for the removal hearing. The respondent thus claims that there were exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. However, the motion to reopen proceedings was untimely. The respondent’s motion was submitted by present counsel on January 24, 2003, far more than 180 days after the Immigration Judge’s February 3, 1998, in absentia decision.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent claims that his former counsel’s ineffectiveness constitutes an exception to the time and numerical bars on motions to reopen, the Board has held that an ineffective assistance claim does not qualify as an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen or rescind an in absentia order of deportation. Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec 113 (BIA 1998). The respondent in his affidavit admits that he was informed of the scheduled hearing date, but he failed to attend due to incorrect statements by an individual in former counsel’s office. The respondent, however, had the obligation to attend the scheduled hearing. See also Bejar v. Ashcroft, 324 F.3d 127 (3d Cir. 2003) (no exceptional circumstances shown where alien’s former counsel was notified of the hearing). The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely and the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion to reopen and the subsequent motion to reconsider.
 
We also agree that the respondent’s motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was not properly made since the respondent failed to comply with the procedural requirements set out in Matter of Lozada. The fact that the respondent subsequently complied with those procedural requirements cannot remedy this fundamental defect. Finally, we have considered the respondent’s affidavit and the allegations he made against his former counsel. We have also considered the affidavit of the respondent’s former counsel, which was filed in response to the respondent’s bar complaint wherein he vigorously disputes the respondent’s allegations against him. See Ponce-Levia v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377 (3d Cir. 2003) (to advance a successful claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate prejudice--he “must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.”). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In the case at hand former counsel’s affidavit has the ring of truth. Former counsel disputes the essential allegations made by the respondent. Former counsel writes that he never told his employee to tell the respondent that he should not appear in court for the scheduled hearing. Former counsel makes a convincing presentation at 4 of his affidavit that it would not have been logical for either himself or his employee who has since retired to negligently tell the respondent not to appear for the scheduled hearing. Former counsel also points out that the respondent received the notice of hearing from the Immigration Court which warned the respondent of the penalties for his failure to appear. In fact a copy of that hearing notice appears in the record of proceeding and is dated January 27, 1998. We find, therefore, that the respondent has not established the element of prejudice in his attempt to establish ineffective assistance of his former counsel. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 848509 (BIA)

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted by Ok Hee Kim v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., July 11, 2006
2004 WL 848513 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OK HEE KIM
File: A75 466 422 - Honolulu
February 27, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lawrence Sizemore, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
June Y.I. Ito
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; remand; waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h); voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s September 10, 2002, denial of her applications for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h) and adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) should have been equitably estopped from placing her into removal proceedings; that she did not receive a full and fair hearing because her motion to change venue was denied and because she received ineffective assistance of counsel and an incompetent interpreter; that section 212(h) of the Act is unconstitutional; and that the Immigration Judge erred in not granting her voluntary departure.
 
The first issue we consider is whether the DHS should have been equitably estopped from placing the respondent into removal proceedings. This Board is without authority to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel against the DHS so as to preclude it from undertaking a lawful course of action that it is empowered to pursue by statute and regulation. Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335 (BIA 1991). The respondent does not allege that the DHS acted beyond its authority. Moreover, it is well within the DHS’s authority and prosecutorial discretion to initiate removal proceedings. Matter of Ramirez-Somera, 20 I&N Dec. 564 (BIA 1992); see also Matter of U-M-, 20 I&N Dec. 327 (BIA 1991); Matter of Torres, 19 I&N Dec. 371 (BIA 1986); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of Geronimo, 13 I&N Dec. 680 (BIA 1971).
 
The second issue we consider is whether the respondent received a full and fair hearing. The respondent claims that the denial of her motion to change venue, the ineffective assistance of her attorneys,1 and an incompetent interpreter deprived her of a full and fundamentally fair hearing in violation of her right to due process.
 
*2 With respect to the motion to change venue, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that motions to change venue are left to the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, but an arbitrary refusal to change venue can be a violation of the statutory right to a reasonable opportunity to attend and present evidence at the deportation hearing. Campos v. Nail, 43 F.3d 1285 (9th Cir. 1994), citing Baires v. INS, 856 F.2d 89, 92 (9th Cir. 1988). In this case, the Immigration Judge did not make an arbitrary decision. The Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s circumstances that her residence and business were in Maryland as was her disabled husband who was supposed to testify on her behalf. Tr. at 11-24. However, on the day the Immigration Judge considered the motion, the respondent and her attorneys were already present in court and were scheduled to proceed with a merits hearing. Also, the Immigration Judge was willing to allow the respondent’s husband to testify via telephone. Thus, the respondent was not deprived of a full and fair hearing due to the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to change venue. We also find that the respondent was not deprived a full and fair hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, the Ninth Circuit has held that to show a deprivation of due process caused by ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must show that counsel’s ineffective performance prejudiced her. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2002). Prejudice results when the “performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id., citing Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
Although the respondent has complied with the Lozada requirements, she cannot show that the performance of her attorneys was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.2 Id. The respondent claims that instead of raising the substantive issue of whether her crimes arose out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct for purposes of removability, her attorneys conceded removability without advising her of the consequences and without her consent. The respondent also claims that she was not advised of the option to seek voluntary departure in lieu of a removal proceeding in order to avoid having to establish good moral character. Finally, the respondent claims that because her attorneys transferred her file to her new attorney in Maryland before the Immigration Judge decided the motion to change venue, they were completely unprepared for the merits hearing and were unable to submit any evidence or documents in support of her claim. Respondent’s Brief at 32-36.
 
*3 We first consider the issue of removability. We find that the failure of the respondent’s attorneys to contest removability at the hearing would not have affected the outcome of the case because the respondent is removable for having committed two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct. Section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act. The Ninth Circuit has held that completed crimes committed on different dates or in different places are presumed to be separate and different crimes not arising out of a single scheme. See Leon-Hernandez v. INS, 926 F.2d 902 (9th Cir. 1991), citing Chanan Din Khan v. Barber, 253 F.2d 547, 548 (9th Cir. 1958). To rebut this presumption an alien must do more than simply present “any evidence” of a common scheme. Id. There must be evidence of a conscious, coherent plan or program of future action. Id. A vague, indeterminate expectation to repeat a prior criminal modus operandi is not enough to establish a single scheme. Id., adopting Nason v. INS, 394 F.2d 223 (2d Cir. 1968).
 
In this case, the respondent was convicted of two counts of prostitution in violation of Hawaii Revised Statute § 712-1200. According to the police report included with the respondent’s brief, the Honolulu Police Department had an ongoing undercover investigation of prostitution and other Morals related offenses at the respondent’s place of employment, Rainbow Toning & Oriental Relaxation Therapy. After offering two different undercover police officers sex for money on two separate days, the respondent was arrested and subsequently convicted. These facts raise the presumption that the crimes did not arise out of a single scheme and this presumption has not been rebutted. There is no evidence that the respondent’s offer of sex for money to two different individuals on two different days was part of a conscious, coherent plan or program of future action. Leon-Hernandez v. INS, supra. While the respondent’s convictions arose out of the single scheme of an ongoing undercover police investigation, her criminal misconduct leading to those convictions was no more than an indeterminate expectation that her customers would be willing to pay her for sex. Leon-Hernandez v. INS, supra. The respondent also argues that her criminal misconduct arose out of a single scheme because she was not given the mandatory sentence of 30 days imprisonment under Hawaii law for subsequent offenses. Respondent’s Brief at 20. We decline to accept this argument. The State’s decision on how to treat the respondent’s crimes for sentencing purposes does not affect the “single scheme” analysis established by the Ninth Circuit for immigration purposes. Accordingly, we find that the respondent is removable for having committed two crimes involving moral turpitude, not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct and that the failure of her attorneys to contest removability did not affect the outcome of her case.
 
*4 We now consider the other bases for the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim. Failure to advise the respondent of the option to seek voluntary departure in lieu of a removal proceeding did not affect the outcome of the proceeding. The respondent does not allege that she would have opted for voluntary departure in lieu of a proceeding if it had been offered. Moreover, all of the evidence in the record shows that the respondent wanted to stay in the United States with her husband. This desire by the respondent suggests that her attorneys made a tactical decision to go forward with the proceeding in order to obtain a waiver of inadmissibility so that the respondent could remain in the United States. Tactical decisions, even if in hindsight unwise, do not constitute ineffective assistance. Thorsteinsson v. INS, 724 F.2d 1365, 1367-68 (9th Cir. 1984). Likewise, the premature decision to transfer the respondent’s file to her new attorney in Maryland does not constitute ineffective assistance. Although the respondent’s attorneys did not have the file for her merits hearing, they had already submitted many documents in support of her claim; both the Immigration Judge and the DHS attorney had these documents in their files. In addition, the Immigration Judge continued the hearing to give her attorneys a chance to retrieve the file. Tr. at 57-58. Finally, all of the documents submitted to support the respondent’s application for a discretionary 212(h) waiver were ultimately moot because she was statutorily ineligible for the waiver. See section 212(h) (stating that a lawful permanent resident cannot be granted a waiver if she has not lawfully resided in the United States for 7 years immediately preceding the initiation of removal proceedings).3 Accordingly, the foregoing actions by the respondent’s attorneys did not affect the outcome of the proceeding. We also find that the respondent was not deprived of a full and fair hearing due to an incompetent interpreter.
 
The interpreter’s errors cited on appeal are not significant. The respondent has not identified any examples of material testimony which was not translated, or explained how the Immigration Judge relied on incorrectly translated testimony in reaching her decision, or demonstrated how a better translation would have made a difference in the outcome of the hearing. Perez-Lastor v. INS, 208 F.3d 773 (9th Cir. 2000); Hartooni v. INS, 21 F.3d 336, 339-40 (9th Cir. 1994); Matter of G-, 20 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1993); Matter of K-L-, 20 I&N Dec. 654 (BIA 1993); Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105, 107-08 (BIA 1984)
 
The third issue we consider is whether section 212(h) violates the Equal Protection Clause of the Constitution. We have repeatedly held that we are not empowered to rule on the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations that we administer. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992); Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 339 (BIA 1991); Matter of Fede, 20 I&N Dec. 35, 36 (BIA 1989); Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343, 345 (BIA 1982); Matter of Cenatice, 16 I&N Dec. 162, 166 (BIA 1977); Matter of L-, 4 I&N Dec. 556, 557 (BIA 1951).
 
*5 The final issue we consider is whether the Immigration Judge erred in denying the respondent voluntary departure. In order to be eligible for voluntary departure at the conclusion of proceedings the alien must show, inter alia, that she is, and has been, a person of good moral character for at least 5 years immediately preceding the alien’s application for voluntary departure. Section 240B(b)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(B). However, as defined, an alien cannot establish good moral character if she is a member of one of the classes of persons described in, inter alia, section 212(a)(2)(D) of the Act, Prostitution and Commercialized Vice. Section 101(f)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(3). The respondent certainly fits within the description of section 212(a)(2)(D) of the Act as a person who has engaged in prostitution within 10 years of the date of application for a visa, admission, or adjustment of status.4 Therefore, by definition, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for voluntary departure and the Immigration Judge did not err in making this finding.
 
Based on the foregoing, we find that the DHS was not equitably estopped from initiating proceedings, the respondent received a full and fundamentally fair hearing, the respondent is removable for having committed two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, we do not have the power to consider the constitutionality of section 212(h) of the Act, and the Immigration Judge did not err in denying her voluntary departure.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>

2004 WL 848557 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TRINIDAD ANTONIA RAMIREZ SALAZAR
File: AXX XX1 576 - Los Angeles
February 24, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on December 9, 2003, when we summarily affirmed without opinion an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 2, 2002, finding her removable, as charged, but granting her the privilege of voluntary departure. On January 12, 2003, the respondent moved this Board to reconsider its decision alleging a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). In her motion, the respondent does not specify the legal or factual defect in our previous decision, and we find no error in our decision that would warrant reconsideration. Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is more properly characterized as a motion to reopen, because the respondent asserts new facts and makes new allegations. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). To the extent the respondent alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel, we note that in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we laid out the requirements that the respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. First, the motion should be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts. Second, before the allegation is presented to the Board, the former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion. Finally, if it is asserted that prior counsel’s handling of the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the established requirements to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. We recognize that the present case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has held that rigid adherence to the procedural requirements established in Lozada is not always necessary. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir 2000). However, where the respondent has failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements and where the respondent has also failed to show any prejudice, we are compelled to deny the motion to reopen. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 848560 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIZABETH BIRU
File: AXX XX5 092 - Arlington
February 24, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jim Tom Haynes, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On August 5, 2003, the respondent filed with this Board a motion to reopen and remand, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion. The unopposed motion will granted.
 
In Matter of Lozada, supra, this Board discussed the criteria which must be present for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel arising in immigration proceedings: (1) the alien must provide an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement entered into with counsel regarding the actions to be taken, and what representations counsel did or did not make in this regard; (2) counsel whose integrity or competence has been impugned must be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her, and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) the evidence of record must reflect whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
In this case, the respondent has complied with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent’s alleges that although she was eligible to adjust her status based on an approved I-130 visa petition, her prior counsel failed to file a motion to remand with the Board during the pendency of her appeal. Furthermore, the respondent’s former counsel did not inform her that her appeal was dismissed and that she had been granted voluntary departure by the Board on November 4, 2002. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board in order to pursue her claim for adjustment of status but the motion was denied by the Board on April 12, 2003 because of the respondent’s failure to comply with the Board’s prior order of voluntary departure. The respondent supports her motion with an affidavit from one former counsel admitting that mistakes were made in the respondent’s case and that Counsel’s failure to act caused the respondent’s grant of voluntary departure to expire, causing substantial prejudice to the respondent. The DHS has raised no objection to respondent’s motion and arguments.
 
Accordingly, we find that the respondent has demonstrated that her failure to depart pursuant to the terms of our November 4, 2002, order was due to exceptional circumstances beyond her control. See generally Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (stating that ineffective assistance of counsel could constitute an exceptional circumstance for an alien’s failure to appear for his hearing). We further find that the respondent has submitted sufficient evidence with her motion to establish prima facie eligibility for the relief she seeks in reopened proceedings, and to demonstrate that the previous procedural defects resulted from the ineffective assistance of prior counsel.
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

2004 WL 848460 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE RUIZ-MARTINEZ
File: AXX XX1 916 - Buffalo
February 23, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frederick P. Korkosz, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On December 29, 2003, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. We have jurisdiction over this timely motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The motion will be denied.
 
The Board originally dismissed the respondent’s appeal on February 9, 2001, for not filing the appeal brief by the April 8, 1998, deadline. The Board reissued its decision on November 20, 2003, as the original order was not properly served. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, argues in his motion that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel when his former counsel neglected to file his appeal brief timely. He also requests reopening so that he may present new and previously unavailable evidence in support of his application for suspension of deportation under section 244(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254(a).
 
We first address the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In his motion to reopen, the respondent neither met the procedural requirements of a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, nor showed that he was prejudiced by his attorney’s failure to file the appeal brief. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); see also, Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108 (2d Cir. 1993). We find no merit in the respondent’s bare assertion, through current counsel, that his former counsel’s failure to file an appeal brief “clearly constitutes ineffective assistance.…” Motion at 2-3, para. 5. In Matter of Assaad, supra, the Board held that an attorney’s failure to file an appeal on behalf of an alien is not per se ineffective assistance of counsel. Instead, in addition to fulfilling the procedural requirements identified in Matter of Lozada, supra, the respondent is required to establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his former attorney’s ineffectiveness. Matter of Assaad, supra, at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Id. This means that the claimant must show thal he likely would have prevailed on appeal had the negligence not occurred. Id. The respondent, through current counsel, has not even attempted to show prejudice. Further, we find no merit in the respondent’s assertion, through current counsel, that he has been unable to “raise¿º” a “proper claim of ineffective assistance of counsel” because he has been unable to determine which of the two former attorneys was responsible for having failed to file the appeal brief. Motion at 2-3, para. 5. The respondent and his current counsel have had since at least August 4, 2003, the date of the filing of current counsel’s Notice of Entry of Appearance before the Board, to arrange with the Board’s Clerk’s Office opportunity to review the record. Board of Immigration Appeals Practice Manual, 1.5(c).
 
*2 Moreover, having reviewed the entire record, we find no prejudice. The record shows that the respondent received a fair and complete hearing before the Immigration Judge, and was provided every opportunity to present his application for suspension of deportation during the proceedings below. At the hearing, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had not demonstrated that his return to Mexico would result in extreme hardship within the meaning of the immigration law (I.J. at 6-9). Nothing in the respondent’s motion convinces us that the respondent likely would have prevailed on appeal of the extreme hardship determination. See Tr. at 13, et. seq.; Exh. 3, Group Exh. 4 (evidence presented below in support of suspension application demonstrating only economic, social, and emotional hardships ordinarily faced by similarly situated deportees, and not other substantial equities such as advanced age or severe illness). Matter of Anderson, 16 I&N Dec. 596 (BIA 1978); Matter of Chumpitazi, 16 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 1978); see also, INS v. Wang, 450 US 139 (1981).
 
Next, we address the respondent’s motion to reopen to present new evidence in support of his application for suspension of deportation. With his motion, the respondent attached evidence of the hardship he and his 8-year-old United States citizen son would likely face upon the respondent’s deportation. In particular, the respondent provided evidence of community ties that he has developed since he came to the United States. He also provided evidence indicating that, were his son to accompany him to Mexico, his son would suffer both economic and social detriment, and, were his son to remain in the United States, his son would likely suffer profound loss. Considering this evidence, both individually and cumulatively, we find that the respondent has not demonstrated that, if his motion to reopen were granted, the new evidence presented would likely change the result of the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
In his motion, the respondent failed to show factors that demonstrate extreme hardship over and above the normal economic and social disruptions involved in deportation. Matter of Kao and Lin, 23 I&N Dec. 45 (BIA 2001); Matter of Pilch, 21 I&N Dec. 627 (BIA 1996); Matter of Anderson, supra. Economic detriment and the loss of social and educational opportunities does not amount to extreme hardship because these hardships are likely to occur in most cases involving the deportation of aliens to comparatively economically depressed countries. We note that there is no evidence demonstrating whether it is the respondent’s intention for his United States citizen son to move to Mexico, or for the child to remain in this country. Matter of Ige, 20 I & N Dec. 880, 885 (1994). We also note that, were the child to go to Mexico, the evidence offered would not change the result of the case. The respondent’s young son would not suffer extreme hardship within the meaning of the immigration law, as the child has some Spanish language skills; he is young and only in elementary school; and there is no evidence offered to indicate physical or mental infirmities that would be of particular disadvantage to the family in Mexico. Matter of Kao and Lin, supra.; Matter of Pilch, supra. For these reasons, we conclude that the respondent did not show that he likely would prevail were the case reopened. Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

2004 WL 848555 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROLEX O’NEIL HINES
File: A41 360 757 - Boston
February 23, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se1
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on July 29, 1998. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not relevant here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before October 27, 1998. The record reflects, however, that the respondent’s motion was not filed with the Board until November 7, 2003. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
The respondent states he never received a copy of the Board’s July 29, 1998 order. A review of the record indicates it was mailed to respondent’s former attorney of record on that date, and that it was not returned as undeliverable by the postal service. To the extent the respondent appears to be making a claim of ineffective assistance, we note that a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10. (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has failed to meet these requirements.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. As the motion to reopen is denied, the motion for a stay of deportation is also denied.

2004 WL 1167227 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICIO JIMENEZ A.K.A. MAURICIO JIMENEZ-ACEVADO
File: A29 647 227 - Denver
February 20, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raul Villasuso, Jr., Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening or reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s October 16, 2003, motion to reopen/reconsider is denied. The respondent contends that the failure of his former counsel to notify him of this Board’s December 27, 2000, summary dismissal of the original appeal amounted to ineffective representation. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Otherwise, the respondent apparently contends, be could have timely filed a motion to reopen to pursue relief under former section 212(c) of Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), in light of the United States Supreme Court decision, INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2.
 
Aside from the question of whether the filing period for motions should be equitably tolled in light of the respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, or in light of the new in interpretation of the law per INS v. St. Cyr, supra, we find that the respondent has failed to show that he has been prejudiced or that he is entitled to any relief. We observe that the respondent does not contend in his motion that his former attorney should have filed the appellate brief that was promised in the Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), nor does the respondent assert that the appeal form contained or should have contained any meaningful argument, even though these were the reasons we previously dismissed the appeal. Notably, the respondent has failed to assert that his prior attorney should have requested any particular relief before the Immigration Judge.
 
The record reflects thin in 1989, the respondent was admitted for permanent residence on a conditional basis. This status was terminated 2 years later, however, for the failure of both the respondent and his spouse to attend an interview in connection with the joint petition to remove the conditional status. The respondent was convicted on June 1, 1994, in Wisconsin for delivery of cocaine. He asserts that he pled guilty to this offense in 1993.
 
The respondent is not a lawful permanent resident, and he has not presented evidence of lawful unrelinquished domicile of 7 years or more. The respondent, therefore, was ineligible for section 212(c) relief at the time of his guilty plea and conviction, and at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, regardless of later changes in the law that affected other applicants for this waiver. Clearly the Immigration Judge did not apply the AEDPA1 in pretermitting the respondent’s 212(c) application since that 1996 enactment did not exist at the time of the Immigration Judge’s 1995 decision. The respondent was simply held to be ineligible for any relief at the time. Thus, any purported ineffective assistance of counsel would have been harmless in light of the respondent’s continued ineligibility for relief.
 
*2 In his motion the respondent may be attempting to blame his former attorney for a bond forfeiture which allegedly resulted from the respondent’s failure to appear for deportation. The respondent did not state that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) failed to notify him of a time and place of departure, i.e. with a so-called “bag and baggage” letter. More importantly, while this Board has appellate jurisdiction over the setting of conditions and the amount of a bond, the question of a forfeited payment to a presumably private bondsman is not an issue over which we have jurisdiction, nor could it properly be part of these deportation proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(b)(7), 1003.19, 1236.1(d)(1)-(4).

2004 WL 1167095 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOMAS REYES MARIA GUADALUPE REYES TOMAS DE JESUS REYES
Files: AXX XX6 099 - Houston
AXX XX6 100
AXX XX6 101
February 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Jose W. Vega, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charlotte K. Lang
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM: This case was last before us on August 22, 2003, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondents’ applications for relief. The respondents have now filed a motion to reopen their removal proceedings, arguing that they were the victims of ineffective assistance of counsel when their former counsel failed to properly put forward their claim for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has filed an opposition to the respondents’ motion. The motion is denied.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondents have satisfied in their motion the procedural requirements set forth in Lozada, supra. However, this Board has held that satisfying the above procedural requirements is in and of itself an insufficient reason to reopen. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). It is also required that the claimants establish that they suffered prejudice as a result of the attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id.
 
In the instant case, the respondents received a fair and complete hearing before the Immigration Judge. They were adequately represented by counsel throughout their proceedings, and were provided every opportunity to present their claim for cancellation of removal. At the hearing, the Immigration Judge determined that the adult respondents had not met their burden of proof with respect to demonstrating that their removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to their United States citizen child or to the female respondent’s lawful permanent resident mother. The Immigration Judge also noted that the minor respondent was not eligible for this form of relief from removal. In addition, the Immigration Judge properly found that all respondents were statutorily ineligible for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b).
 
*2 The respondents’ motion does not present the contents of the evidence or testimony they allege was not presented due to their counsel’s ineffective assistance. Therefore, the respondents cannot establish that this evidence would have met their burden of proof for cancellation of removal, and that they were prejudiced by its absence. Consequently, we cannot reopen these proceedings. Matter of Assaad, supra. The motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1167089 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO GARC1A-RODRIGUEZ VERONICA GARCIA-MEDINA JONATHAN ESPINOZA-MEDINA
Files: AXX XX2 315 - Los Angeles
AXX XX2 316
AXX XX2 245
February 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Richard Fraade, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge denied the respondents cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), as being unable to show that removal would cause “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” to the United States citizen children of the adult respondents. We dismissed the respondents’ appeal on June 12, 2002, and denied a motion to reconsider this decision on December 9, 2002. On November 21, 2003, the respondents filed a “Motion to Vacate And Reinstate Previous Order Dated June 12, 2002”, which we will construe as a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondents allege that their former representatives failed to file a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals, but instead submitted the unsuccessful motion to reconsider. They therefore seek to have the Board reinstate its June 12, 2002, decision. The respondents have not met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Specifically, they fail to show prejudice resulting from their representatives’ actions, as the Ninth Circuit lacked jurisdiction to review a discretionary determination to deny cancellation of removal. Romero-Torres v. Ashcroft, 327 F.3d 887 (9th Cir. 2003). As the court stated, supra, at 888, “… we conclude that an “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” determination is a subjective, discretionary judgment that has been carved out of our appellate jurisdiction.” Their representatives’ alleged failure to pursue a petition for review in the Ninth Circuit therefore caused the respondents no prejudice. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

2004 WL 1167080 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN CARLOS JIMENEZ-VALLE
File: A77 539 673 - Seattle
February 18, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert Gibbs, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eric Bakken
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The record is remanded. This case was last before us on December 14, 2001, when we remanded the record to permit the respondent FN;B1[FN1]co_anchor_FN_F1_94 to apply for an adjustment of status based upon his approved labor certification (Form I-140). We remanded at that time to permit the respondent to file his application for adjustment (Form I-485).
 
On remand, the respondent failed to meet the Immigration Judge’s filing deadline of March 18, 2002, for the adjustment application, and the respondent was ordered removed on May 16, 2002. The respondent did not file an appeal of that decision because he was apparently unaware of it.
 
In May 2003, the respondent moved to reopen proceedings before the Immigration Judge, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel; it was asserted that a California law firm, which the respondent had retained, failed to timely file the adjustment application.2 See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (regarding the requirements to properly set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (Lozada requirements are “generally reasonable” but not “sacrosanct”); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 559, n.6 (BIA 2003) (reaffirming the standards set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and noting that the Ninth Circuit has adopted a flexible approach to the Lozada requirements). In a May 28, 2003, decision denying the motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge found, inter alia, that the respondent had failed to meet the Lozada requirements because he did not provide the accused counsel in question with any opportunity to respond to the allegations, nor had the respondent filed a complaint with the state bar concerning this allegedly inadequate representation. The motion was also denied as being untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent appealed.
 
In analyzing claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, the Ninth Circuit has recognized that “equitable tolling” of deadlines may occur where an alien is prevented from filing a claim for relief because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the alien acts with “due diligence” in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003); see also Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
*2 The record reflects that the respondent worked actively in March 2002 to present his application for adjustment with all the necessary paperwork. We find that this reflects due diligence. The law firm in question that was supposed to file the application was repeatedly notified by the respondent’s counsel of record, Kaaren Barr, about the urgent need to meet the filing deadline set by the Immigration Judge. While we are concerned that the respondent perhaps did not maintain enough contact with his counsel of record or the Immigration Court, he did appear to be working quite diligently with the law firm which was to file his paperwork. He had every reason to believe that he was appropriately and timely filing his adjustment of status application. He was also apparently advised by that firm that it would handle his application and that he need not work with Ms. Barr toward that end.
 
The respondent is correct in his assertion that the California law firm’s failure to timely file such an application for relief—particularly given the respondent’s cooperation with the firm and its apparent knowledge of the deadline—is ineffective assistance of counsel. The purported failure of the respondent to provide an exact amount for a filing fee—he provided $70 in excess—is insufficient reason for the application not to have been timely filed and does not explain the long delay even after this problem was apparently cured. Pursuant to the previously cited Ninth Circuit case law, the respondent is correct that these circumstances adequately set forth a claim of ineffective assistance without the necessity of a bar complaint or a complaint to the counsel of record. The respondent was highly prejudiced by the California law firm’s failure to file, and there is no evidence of record that would excuse the firm’s failure to do so. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra; Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, supra; Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, supra; Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra.
 
In light of the foregoing, the deadline to file the adjustment application was tolled. But for the California firm’s ineffective assistance, the respondent should not have been in a position where he would need to file a motion to reopen. Moreover, that firm had an additional obligation, whether it was counsel of record or not, to advise him that it filed the application late. Its failure to do so further prejudiced the respondent’s interests. Accordingly, the deadline to file the motion to reopen was also tolled.
 
We also do not uphold the Immigration Judge’s application of the “fugitive disentitlement doctrine,” which does not appear to be applicable to this case. Cf. Antonio-Martinez v. INS, 317 F.3d 1089 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
As it now appears that the respondent is prima facie eligible to adjust his status, we will reopen proceedings, and remand the record for consideration of his adjustment application. The respondent is not precluded from seeking any other relief for which he may be entitled.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings in accordance with the foregoing opinion and for the entrY of a new decision.

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen before the Board. We last addressed this case on April 10, 2003, when we summarily affirmed an earlier order of an Immigration Judge. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reopening to pursue adjustment of status. See section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. To support her motion, the respondent initially provided proof that an employer filed an application for a labor certification on her behalf which was dated April 9, 2001. On August 11, 2003, the respondent provided evidence that the labor certification was approved on August 3, 2003.
 
The respondent’s motion is deficient as it does not include a completed Application to Register Permanent Residence or Adjust Status (Form I-485), or any other documentation outside of the labor certification which indicates that she is otherwise eligible for adjustment of status. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1)(“a motion to reopen for the purpose of submitting an application must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.”); see also Matter of Yewondwosen, 21 I&N Dec. 1025 (BIA 1997). We point out that on July 28, 2003, the Department of Homeland Security (formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) filed a response to the motion which specifically objected to the motion on this basis. The respondent did submit the approved labor certification after this date, but failed to remedy the other defects in her motion. Therefore, she has not met the requirements for filing a motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion also fails to include an approved visa petition, or any evidence that a petition is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(g). The respondent’s motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status will therefore be denied.
 
In her motion, the respondent asserts that her appellate counsel was ineffective. However, she only supports this by attaching a copy of the appeal brief. We have no statement from the respondent herself, or a clear indication of how she was ultimately prejudiced by the conduct of her counsel, or how this claim should impact the adjudication of her motion to roepen. The respondent has therefore not met her burden of proving that any reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879 (2 nd Cir. 1994); cf. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (discussing requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 We conclude that the motion to reopen should be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 1167078 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FALEFATU ASI
File: A37 046 232 - Los Angeles
February 18, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Paz Soldan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on November 17, 2003, when this Board dismissed as untimely the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reconsider. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider asserts that at the time of the Board’s previous decision an error was made. It “questions the Board’s decision for alleged errors in appraising the facts and the law.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). We note that the respondent in this case has presented no error of law or fact in the Board’s prior decision. Instead the respondent makes a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel,1 explaining that the notice of appeal was sent Federal Express mail to the Board on September 25, 2003, one day before it was due at the Board. Due to a typographical error in the zip code, respondent’s appeal was not delivered to the Board the next day.
 
A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). While counsel for respondent has filed an affidavit acknowledging fault on his part, the respondent has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary committee for the state of California. We also note, that despite this claim of ineffective assistance, the respondent has continued to utilize the services of the attorney in question.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

A review of the record indicates that respondent’s appeal was filed pro se.

*1 PER CURIAM. The Board will return the record to the Immigration Judge for preparation of a complete record.
 
On February 24, 2000, at a scheduled hearing conducted in absentia, the Immigration Judge found the respondent to be removable from the United States. The respondent filed a motion to reopen claiming that he did not receive notice of the hearing, and a supplemental motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. On November 9, 2000, the Immigration Judge denied reopening, and the respondent filed an appeal from that decision.
 
Upon consideration of the appeal, we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. We found that the notice of the February 24, 2000, hearing was sent to an attorney who formerly represented the respondent. The record, however, did not contain a Notice of Entry of Appearance before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) from that attorney. In our decision, we stressed that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented. See sections 239(a)(1) and 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1) and 1229a(b)(5); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Inasmuch as notice should have been provided to the respondent himself as an unrepresented alien, we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge on October 28, 2002, for further proceedings.
 
On March 21, 2003, the Immigration Judge certified this case back to the Board. The Immigration Judge stated that “the entire record of proceedings was not located before the record was submitted to the Board … (and) did not include the Form EOIR-28 from the respondent’s (former) counsel” (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge further states that “¿tºo supplement the now complete record, a copy of the Form EOIR-28 submitted by the initial attorney is attached to (the March 21, 2003) Order” (I.J. at 2). In the record now before us, however, the Form EOIR-28 at issue is not attached to the Immigration Judge’s decision. As we consider the Form EOIR-28 from respondent’s former counsel to be necessary for our review of this matter, we will return the record to the Immigration Court for further action. Upon receipt of the record, the Immigration Court shall take such steps as are necessary and appropriate to enable preparation of a complete record, beginning with the Notice to Appear, and to ensure that the record is complete before certifying the case to the Board. Accordingly, the record is returned to the Immigration Judge for further action as deemed appropriate.1

2004 WL 1167215 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GORDON, ONIEL
File: AXX-XX3-407 - OAKDALE
February 12, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro Se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated December 15, 2003. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2), a motion to reconsider a decision must be filed with the Board within 30 days after the mailing of the Board decision or on or before July 31, 1996, whichever is later. The record reflects that on December 15, 2003, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the decision of an Immigration Judge. Because the respondent’s motion to reconsider was filed more than 30 days after the mailing of the Board’s prior decision, it is untimely. We have considered the respondent’s argument that the Board should accept his motion to reconsider based upon his contention that his former attorney was negligent. According to the respondent, his former attorney fold him only a week before his motion to reconsider was due that his appeal was dismissed. The respondent claimed that his attorney’s dereliction of duty caused him to be unable to file his motion on time.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The Board has also held that satisfying the above procedural requirements is in and of itself an insufficient reason to reopen, and that an attorney’s failure to file an appeal on behalf of an alien is not per se ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Id. This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. In Matter of Assaad, supra, the Board indicated that an attorney’s failure to file an appeal brief on behalf of an alien is not per se negligence.
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent has not complied with the above requirements. His complaint filed against his attorney does not address the failure to file the motion to reconsider. Rather, it addresses the failure to file a brief in support of the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision. We further find no error in the Board’s prior order in this matter even had the motion been timely filed. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2004 WL 1167219 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LOURDES GARCIA RAMOS A.K.A. MARIA DE LOURDES GARCIA RAMOS
File: A77 843 699 - Council Bluffs
February 11, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James A. Benzoni, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening and remand
 
In a decision dated February 19, 2001, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia after she failed to appear for a removal hearing. On November 13, 2002, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. In a decision dated December 11, 2002, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen. The respondent timely filed an appeal, as well as a motion to remand or reopen, alleging that she had received ineffective assistance of counsel from her prior attorney. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, entered the United States without inspection on October 21, 1988. On July 19, 2000, the DHS served the respondent with a Notice to Appear. In a letter dated September 18, 2001, mailed to the respondent’s attorney at the time, she was notified of the hearing scheduled on February 19, 2002. The respondent has acknowledged that she received notice of this hearing but claims that she was unable to attend the hearing because her vehicle broke down while she was on her way to the hearing. However, the Immigration Judge, having been provided with no reasonable cause for why the respondent was absent from the hearing, conducted it in absentia and found the respondent removable as charged. She was ordered removed to Mexico.
 
With the assistance of her former counsel, Rufino Villarreal, a motion to reopen was filed on November 13, 2002, alleging that the respondent had had transportation problems on her way to Court and that was the reason why she missed the February 19, 2002 hearing. A car repair bill was submitted with the motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposed the motion, arguing that it had not been filed in a timely manner within the 180-day period following the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
On December 11, 2002, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondent had failed to meet her burden of establishing that exceptional circumstances had prevented her from attending the hearing. The Immigration Judge noted that the evidence submitted, a mechanics bill, was dated March 16, 2002, nearly a month after the hearing date and did not demonstrate the alleged vehicle problems on the date of the hearing. The respondent secured the assistance of another attorney, who filed an appeal on January 3, 2003, alleging that the respondent had received ineffective assistance from her former counsel. Additional evidence with regard to the respondent’s vehicle breakdown on February 19, 2002, was submitted. In addition, evidence was submitted as to the ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former counsel. The respondent’s attorney also submitted a motion to remand on January 21, 2003.
 
*2 The respondent argues ineffective assistance of counsel for several reasons, including that the prior attorney failed to file the respondent’s initial motion to reopen in a timely manner. The evidence shows that the respondent’s initial motion to reopen was not filed within the 180-day limit provided for under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The Immigration Judge’s decision was dated February 19, 2002, and the motion was not filed until November 13, 2002. Accordingly, the issue is whether a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel constitutes an exception to, or results in equitable tolling of, this 180-day time limit.
 
Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act expressly requires that a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia deportation order based on exceptional circumstances be filed within 180 days of the order. Because the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed well beyond the 180 days allotted by the statute, we find that she is statutorily barred from rescinding the removal order.
 
The respondent basically argues that there should be an exception to this 180-day rule where the failure to timely file a motion to reopen is due to the ineffective assistance of counsel. Initially, we note that there appears to be no question that the respondent has properly set forth her claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). As the respondent has noted, the Executive Office for Immigration Review and the Board have issued disciplinary actions against the respondent’s former attorney. However, as egregious as the prior attorney’s conduct in this case, the Board has held that such a claim does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation, which is now referred to as removal. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998). The language of section 240(b)(5)(c) of the Act regarding the time limit within which a motion to reopen must be filed is clear on its face and unambiguous, and the statute contains no exceptions to this time bar. See Matter of A-A-, supra; Matter of Lei, supra.1 Had Congress intended to provide for an exception to the 180-day time limit based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, it could have done so. The Board has found that the legislative history demonstrates that the 180-day limit reflects congressional intent to bring finality to in absentia deportation (or removal) proceedings. See id.
 
Therefore, given that the statute is explicit in its requirement that a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the in absentia removal order, and considering the legislative history of the language now found at 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, we continue to hold that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not an exception to the 180-day time limit imposed by that section of the Act. Accordingly, we find that the respondent is statutorily barred from rescinding the order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, regardless of whether she has demonstrated that the failure to timely file the motion to reopen is attributable to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 We note that the hearing in this case was held in Omaha, Nebraska, and the precedent of the United States Court of Appeals, Eighth Circuit, applies in this case. It does not appear that the Eighth Circuit has previously addressed the issue of whether the 180-day period for filing a motion to reopen should be equitably tolled where the petitioner alleges that ineffective assistance of counsel caused the delay in filing such a motion. Other circuit courts that have addressed the issue are split. See Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999) (holding that INA § 242B(c)(3)(A) sets forth a “mandatory and jurisdictional” time bar); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (holding that INA § 242B(c)(3)(A)’s time bar is not jurisdictional and thus subject to equitable tolling); Lavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000) (holding that the equitable tolling doctrine applies to the 90-day period provided in 8 C.F.R. § 3.23(b)(1)(2000) for filing motions to reopen based on “new facts”). The Ninth Circuit has held that the limitation period on a motion to reopen an in absentia deportation order based on exceptional circumstances was equitably tolled until the alien was aware of the harm resulting from the misconduct of her representative. Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
In the present case, we have no binding precedent from the Eighth Circuit or the Board that “equitable tolling” applies to the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, because the respondent’s initial motion to reopen was filed in an untimely manner and she has not shown “exceptional circumstances,” the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated December 11, 2002, denying that motion to reopen, is dismissed.
 
As for the motion to remand, the requirements for such a motion are, for all practical purposes, the same as those for a motion to reopen. See Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880, 884 (BIA 1994). The respondent has submitted additional evidence but it pertains to the underlying motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia deportation order. Based on the analysis above, remanding this case for the Immigration Judge to review the additional evidence would be a futile exercise because even if the additional evidence supports a finding of ineffective assistance of counsel, the initial motion to reopen was not timely filed and ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute “exceptional circumstances” on which to grant a motion on reopen. Therefore, the motion to remand is denied.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009 (Sept. 30, 1996). ** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMENUL HOQE
File: AXX XX7 421 - Chicago
February 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael J. Hernandez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karl V. Cozad
Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal will be dismissed. The record reflects that the respondent waived appeal. The summary of the Immigration Judge’s oral decision, as well as the transcribed order, dated August 29, 2002, noted that the respondent had waived appeal. We also note that the transcript of proceedings, page 8, lines 9-10, reflects that then counsel for the respondent waived appeal. On September 27, 2002, the respondent filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26). Upon review, we find that this case is not properly before us as the respondent has failed to establish that the decision to waive appeal was not a knowing and intelligent one.
 
Counsel for the respondent asserts that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) did not object to prior counsel’s motion for a continuance or administrative closure of the proceedings held August 29, 2002. However, the DHS did oppose the motion, both in writing and on the record (Tr. at 5-6). See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 480 (BIA 1996) (proceedings may not be administratively closed if such closure is opposed by either party), and cases cited therein. Further, counsel argues that the respondent’s prior counsel waived appeal without first consulting the respondent. However, the record reveals that counsel went off the record to discuss the respondent’s options with him (Tr. at 7-8), and indeed the respondent states as much in the affidavit accompanying his appeal brief. The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance from prior counsel, but he has failed to meet any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel), or otherwise to establish such a claim. Here, counsel for the respondent agreed to waive appeal in order to receive a grant of 120 days voluntary departure, pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (INA). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b), (e). The respondent has not shown that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent.
 
Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for stay of removal pending adjudication of his appeal is denied as moot in light of the above decision.

FOR THE BOARD
